Chapter 1 Introductory Material 1 
1.1 Scope 2 
This Technical Specification has been produced by the O-RAN.org. 3 
The contents of the present document are subject to continuing work within O-RAN WG4 and may change following formal 4 
O-RAN approval. Should the O-RAN.org modify the contents of the present document, it will be re -released by O-RAN 5 
Alliance with an identifying change of release date and an increase in version number as follows: 6 
Release x.y.z 7 
where: 8 
x the first digit is incremented for all changes of substance, i.e., technical enhancements, corrections, updates, etc. (the 9 
initial approved document will have x=01). 10 
y the second digit is incremented when editorial only changes have been incorporated in the document. 11 
z the third digit included only in working versions of the document indicating incremental changes during the editing 12 
process. 13 
The present document specifies the management plane protocols used over the fronthaul interface linking the O-RU (O-RAN 14 
Radio Unit) with other management plane entities, that may include the O-DU (O-RAN Distributed Unit), the O-RAN defined 15 
Service Management and Orchestration (SMO) functionality as well as other generic Network Management Systems (NMS).  16 
1.2 References 17 
The following documents contain provisions which, through reference in this text, constitute provisions of the present document. 18 
- References are either specific (identified by date of publication, edition number, version number, etc.) or non-specific. 19 
- For a specific reference, subsequent revisions do not apply. 20 
- For a non-specific reference, the latest version applies. In the case of a reference to a 3GPP document (including a GSM 21 
document), a non-specific reference implicitly refers to the latest version of that document in Release 15. 22 
[1] 3GPP TR 21.905: “Vocabulary for 3GPP Specifications” 23 
[2] ORAN-WG4.CUS.0-v07 “Control, User and Synchronization Plane Specification”, O-RAN  Alliance, 24 
Working Group 4  25 
[3] RFC 6241, “Network Configuration Protocol (NETCONF)”, IETF, June 2011 26 
[4] RFC 7950, “The YANG 1.1 Data Modeling Language”, IETF, August 2016 27 
[5] RFC 6242, “Using the NETCONF Protocol over Secure Shell (SSH)”, IETF, June 2011 28 
[6] RFC 4252, “The Secure Shell (SSH) Authentication Protocol”, IETF, January 2006 29 
[7] RFC 4253, “The Secure Shell (SSH) Transport Layer Protocol”, IETF, January 2006 30 
[8] RFC 2132, “DHCP Options and BOOTP Vendor Extensions”, IETF, March 1997 31 
[9] RFC 3925 , “Vendor -Identifying Vendor Options for Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol version 4 32 
(DHCPv4)”, IETF, October 2004 33 
[10] RFC 2131, “Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol”, IETF, March 1997 34 
[11] RFC 4862, “IPv6 Stateless Address Autoconfiguration”, IETF, September 2007 35 
[12] RFC 3315, “Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol for IPv6 (DHCPv6)”, IETF, July 2003 36 
[13] RFC 3736, “Stateless Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol (DHCP) Service for IPv6”, IETF, April 2004 37 
[14] RFC 8572, “Secure Zero Touch Provisioning (SZTP)”, IETF, April 2019 38 
[15] RFC 8071, “NETCONF Call Home and RESTCONF Call Home”, IETF, February 2017 39

1.3 Definitions and Abbreviations 18 
1.3.1 Definitions 19 
For the purposes of the present document, the terms and definitions given in 3GPP TR 21.905 [1] and the following apply. A 20 
term defined in the present document takes precedence over the definition of the same term, if any, in 3GPP TR 21.905 [1]. 21 
Antenna Line: connection between O-RU and antenna 22 
C-Plane: Control Plane: refers specifically to real-time control between O-DU and O-RU, and should not be confused with the 23 
UE’s control plane 24 
Cascade mode: Mode of Shared cell which is realized by several O-RUs cascaded in chain topology 25 
DL: DownLink: data flow towards the radiating antenna (generally on the LLS interface) 26 
eAxC: extended Antenna-Carrier: a data flow for a single antenna (or spatial stream) for a single carrier in a single sector. 27 
Event-Collector: A REST server to which an O-RU supporting NON -PERSISTENT-NETCONF feature can send a JSON 28 
notification 29 
FHM mode: Mode of Shared cell which is realized by FHM and several O-RUs in star topology.  30 
LLS: Lower Layer Split: logical interface between  O-DU and O-RU when using a  lower layer (intra-PHY based) functional 31 
split. 32 
LLS-U: Lower Layer Split User-plane: logical interface between O-DU and O-RU when using a lower layer functional split. 33 
LLS-C: Lower Layer Split Control-plane: logical interface between O-DU and O-RU when using a lower layer functional split. 34 
LLS-S: Lower Layer Split Synchronization -plane: logical interface between O-DU and O-RU when using a lower layer 35 
functional split. 36 
High-PHY: those portions of the PHY processing on the O-DU side of the fronthaul interface, including FEC encode/decode, 37 
scrambling, and modulation/demodulation. 38 
Low-PHY: those portions of the PHY processing on the O-RU side of the fronthaul interface, including F FT/iFFT, digital 39 
beamforming, and PRACH extraction and filtering. 40 
M-Plane: Management Plane: refers to non-real-time management operations between the O-DU and the O-RU 41 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          14 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
North-node: the O-DU or a connected O-RU closer to the O-DU for the O-RU, e.g., the cascade O-RU#1 connected to O-1 
RU#2 is north-node for O-RU#2, when O-DU, O-RU#1 and O-RU#2 are in cascade chain topology. The O-DU in star 2 
topology connected to an FHM is north-node for the FHM.  3 
NMS: A Network Management System dedicated to O-RU operations 4 
Port: End of a transport link – in most cases this is an optical port 5 
Port Number: A number which identifies a port (see Port). In case of SFP/SFP+ port, port number value is 0 to N -1 where N 6 
is number of ports in the device. Numbers 0 to N-1 are assigned to ports in order following order of labels on the device (labels 7 
for ports are not necessarily numbers starting from zero) 8 
O-DU: O-RAN Distributed Unit: a logical node hosting PDCP/RLC/MAC/High-PHY layers based on a lower layer functional 9 
split. 10 
O-RU: O-RAN Radio Unit: a logical node hosting Low-PHY layer and RF processing based on a lower layer functional split.  11 
This is similar to 3GPP’s “TRP” or “RRH” but more specific in including the Low-PHY layer (FFT/iFFT, PRACH extraction).   12 
O-RU Controller: A network function that is permitted to control the configuration of an O-RU. Examples of O-RU controllers 13 
include, an O-DU, a classical NMS, an O-RAN Service Management and Orchestration function, or other network automation 14 
platforms.  15 
S-Plane: Synchronization Plane: refers to traffic between the O-RU or O-DU to a synchronization controller which is generally 16 
an IEEE-1588 Grand Master (however, Grand Master functionality may be embedded in the O-DU). 17 
Shared cell: The operation for the same cell by several O-RUs.  18 
Shared cell network: the network for several cascade O-RUs in a chain topology or the network for one FHM and several O-19 
RUs in a star topology.  20 
South-node: a connected O-RU far from O-DU for the O-RU, e.g., the cascade O-RU#2 connected to O-RU#1 is south-node 21 
for O-RU#1, when O-DU, O-RU#1 and O-RU#2 are in cascade chain topology. The O-RU in star topology connected to an 22 
FHM is south-node for the FHM. 23 
Spatial stream: the data flow on the DL associated with precoded data (may be same as layers or different if there is expansion 24 
in the precoding), and on UL associated with the number of outputs from the digital beamforming (sometimes called “beams”).  25 
SSM: Synchronization Status Message: part of ITU G.781 and G.8264 standards. 26 
TRX: Refers to the specific processing chain in an O-RU associated with D/A or A/D converters.  Due to digital beamforming 27 
the number of TRXs may exceed the number of spatial streams, and due to analog beamforming, the number of TRXs may be 28 
lower than the number of antenna elements. 29 
U-Plane: User Plane: refers to IQ sample data transferred between O-DU and O-RU 30 
UL: Up-Link: data flow away from the radiating antenna (generally on the LLS interface) 31 
Virtual Connection : a connection between O-RU and O-RU controller. This connection is established by means of 32 
autodetection procedure and is supervised by supervision procedure. 33

1.4 Conventions 36 
This management plane specification includes cross references to a set of associated YANG models. Text may reference 37 
particular YANG leafs, notifications and remote procedure calls (RPCs). In order to assist in readability, all cross references to 38 
YANG defined elements will keep the identical case format as defined in the corresponding YANG model, with the font-weight 39 
set to bold. This convention applies only to text and not to YANG elements embedded into figures.  40

1.6 Revision and Compatibility Handling 13 
The revision statement in the YANG models will be used to describe future revisions to the models that are backwards 14 
compatible, where backwards compatibility changes follow the rules defined in section 11 of RFC 7950 [4] . Backwards 15 
incompatible changes will be addressed by incrementing the number used as part of the model name and namespace, effectively 16 
creating a new YANG model. The format of the namespace used in all O-RAN YANG models is “urn: o-ran:”<model-17 
name>“:”<model-number>, where the initial <model -number> used in a newly defined YANG model is “1.0”.  Where this 18 
document makes reference to models, irrespective of their backward compatibility, a generic <model-number> of “x.y” is used 19 
to enable reference to all versions of the namespace for a particular <model-name>. 20 
 21 
The revision statement in all YANG models includes a reference statement used to cross -reference to the first version of this 22 
document where the corresponding description was introduced . For example, the reference in all revision statements f or the 23 
initial O-RAN models include cross-reference to “ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v01.00”.  24 
 25 
The revision statement of the YANG models also includes a description which is used to track the versioning of the YANG 26 
model. All revision statement descriptions will begin w ith “version ”<a>“.”<b>“.”<c>, where <a>, <b> and <c> are used to 27 
reflect the version of the YANG model, where 28 
 29 
<a> corresponds to the first digit of the O-RAN WG4 management plane specification version where the 30 
corresponding description was first introduced, corresponding to <x> in sub-section 1.1 31 
<b>  is incremented when errors in the YANG model have been corrected 32 
<c> is incremented only in working versions of the YANG model indicating incremental changes during the editing 33 
process 34 
 35 
Note, O-RU Controllers that receive YANG library information from the O-RU with a module revision that is a higher version 36 
than the module revision currently used by the O -RU Controller can assume that models with the same namespace have been 37 
updated to ensure backwards compa tibility. The O -RU Controller can continue to use its current module version and any 38 
unknown schema nodes received from the O -RU, i.e., those introduced in later revisions, should be ignored by the O -RU 39 
Controller. 40 
 41 
1.7 Namespace Compatibility Handling 42 
If backwards incompatible changes have been made, the <model-number> used in the YANG model namespace shall be 43 
incremented. Following such changes, an O -RU may include multiple backwards incompatible namespaces in its YANG 44 
library, for example “urn:o-ran:”<model-name>“:1.0” and “urn:o-ran:”<model-name>“:2.0”. 45 
The O -RAN Adopter License Agreement in Chapter ZZZ defines terms regarding the modification of O -RAN defined 46 
specifications. When such modifications are necessary, the preferred approach for realizing such is for the third-party licensee 47 
to publish their own augmentations to the O -RAN defined YANG models and procedures. An O -RU that supports such third-48 
party modifications shall include such model augmentations in its YANG library. Consequently, an O-RU Controller should be 49 
prepared to ignore any unknown models, e.g., developed according to such a procedure. 50

Chapter 2 High Level Description 1 
2.1 Top level functional description, terminology, including hybrid, 2 
hierarchical 3 
2.1.1 Architecture for O-RAN WG4 Fronthaul functional split 4 
This O-RAN FH specification addresses the lower layer functional split as depicted in Figure 1. Refer to the O-RAN CUS plane 5 
specification [2] for more details on the split architecture. The Lower-Layer Split M-plane (LLS-M) facilitates the initialization, 6 
configuration and management of the O-RU to support the stated functional split. 7 
 8 
Figure 1 – O-RAN WG4 FH functional split 9 
2.1.2 M-Plane architecture model 10 
A NETCONF/YANG based M-Plane is used for supporting the management features including “start up” installation, software 11 
management, configuration management, performance management, fault management and file management towards the O-12 
RU. The M-Plane supports two architectural models: 13 
1. Hierarchical model.  As shown on the left side Figure 1, the O-RU is managed entirely by one or more O-DU(s) using 14 
a NETCONF based M-Plane interface. When the O-RU is managed by multiple O-DUs, it is typically for enabling O-DU 15 
and/or transport connectivity redundancy capabilities. Refer to Chapter 3 for more details. 16 
2. Hybrid model . As shown on the right side of Figure 1, the hybrid architecture enables one or more direct logical 17 
interface(s) between management system (s) and O-RU in addition to a logical interface between O-DU and the O-RU. It 18 
should be noted that the NETCONF clients connecting to the O-RU may be of different classes (e.g. O-DU and SMO). For 19 
example, functions like O-RU software management, performance management, configuration management and fault 20 
management can be managed directly by the management system(s).  21 
In the hybrid model, the O-RU has end to end IP layer connectivity with the SMO. From a physical network point of view, this 22 
connectivity could be via the O-DU, where the O-DU is acting as an IP/Ethernet packet forwarder, forwards the packets between 23 
O-RU and the SMO. Direct logical commu nication between a n O-RU and SMO can be enabled via O-RUs being assigned 24 


                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          18 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
routable IPs or local private IPs resolved by a NAT function in the network (or implemented at the O-DU). Refer to chapter 3 1 
for details how O-RU acquires the IP address of O-DU and SMO for the M-plane communication. 2 
As described in Chapter 3, there is no explicit signaling to indicate that an O-RU is operating in a hierarchical or hybrid 3 
configuration. All NETCONF servers supporting this M -Plane specification shall support multiple NETCONF sessions, and 4 
hence all compliant O-RUs shall be able to support both hierarchical and hybrid deployment. 5 
 6 
Figure 2 - M-Plane Architecture 7 
NETCONF/YANG is used as the network element management protocol [3] and data modeling language [4]. Use of such a 8 
standardized framework and common modeling language simplifies integration between O-DU and O-RU as well as operator 9 
network integration (in terms of running service) in case of elements sharing a common set of capabilities . The framework 10 
supports integration of products with differing capabilities enabled by well-defined published data models. NETCONF also 11 
natively supports a hybrid architecture whi ch enables multiple clients to subscribe and receive information originating at the 12 
NETCONF server in the O-RU. 13 
2.1.3 Transport Network 14 
Based on the transport topology, various modes of network connectivity are possible between O-RU and O-DU and SMO.  15 
The basic requirement for M-Plane is to have end to end IP connectivity between the O-RU and the elements managing it (O-16 
DU, SMO, or so called “O-RU Controllers”). The connectivity between the O-DU and SMO and its management plane are not 17 
in scope of this specification. The O-RU shall support either IPv4 or IPv6 and optionally support dual stack (IPv4 and IPv6). 18 
Note, in previous versions of this specification, only IPv4 was mandatory. In order to ensure backwards compatibility 19 
with equipment supporting earlier versions of this specification, an operator and vendor can agree to use a common IP  20 
version in the O-RU, O-DU and any other O-RU controllers. 21 


                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          19 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
2.1.4 M-Plane functional description 1 
The M-Plane provides the following major functionalities to the O-RU. These features are implemented using the NETCONF 2 
provided functions. 3 
 “Start up” installation  4 
During startup, the O-RU acquires its network layer parameters either via static (pre -configured in the O-RU) or dynamically 5 
via DHCP or DHCPv6. During this process the O-RU may acquire the IP address of the O-RU controller(s), in which case the 6 
O-RU establishes the NETCONF connectivity using the “call home” feature. When the O-RU is operating in an environment 7 
which include the O-RAN defined SMO, the O-RU may acquire the IP address of the event -collector(s), in which case the O-8 
RU performs a pnfRegistration which triggers the SMO to establish NETCONF connectivity using the information recovered 9 
from the pnfRegistration procedure.  The capability exchange is performed between the client and server as part of the initial 10 
NETCONF Hello exchanges. Details of these steps are provided in chapter 3. 11 
Note, the use of “start up” terminology in this specification is distinct from the “startup” capability used in a NETCONF 12 
environment to indicate that a device supports separate running and startup configuration datastores. This specification makes 13 
specific reference to configuration which is required to be stored in “reset persistent memory”. The O-RU shall use this stored 14 
configuration as its “startup” configuration. 15 
SW management  16 
The M-Plane is responsible for software download, installation, validation and activation of new SW when requested by O-RU 17 
Controller. The software download is triggered by NETCONF RPC procedures, and the actual software package download is  18 
performed using sFTP with SSH or FTPES [54] with TLS.  19 
Configuration management 20 
Configuration management covers various scenarios like Retrieve Resource State, Modify Resource State, Modify Parameters 21 
and Retrieve Parameters. NETCONF get-config and edit-config RPCs shall be used for configuration parameter retrieval and 22 
updates at the O-RU  23 
Performance management 24 
Performance management describes the measurements and counters used to collect data related to O-RU operations. The 25 
purpose of Performance Management is optimizing the operation of the O-RU.  26 
The measurement results are reported by two options:  27 
1. YANG Notification: This option uses the stats definition of YANG model per measurement group. In this case, get 28 
rpc and/or notification will be used (see Chapter 7 for more details).  29 
2. File Upload: This option uses the file upload procedure defined in File management. The measurement results are 30 
saved to a data file periodically.  31 
Fault Management 32 
Fault management is responsible for sending alarm notif ications to the NETCONF Client. F ault Management allows alarm 33 
notifications to be disabled or enabled as well as alarm subscription. 34 
File Management 35 
File management allows the O-RU Controller to trigger an O-RU to perform upload of files stored on O-RU to O-RU Controller. 36 
The O-RU may provide different kinds of files and retrieved files can be used for various purposes. Simultaneous multiple file 37 
upload operations can be supported under the same sFTP or FTPES [54] connection between O-RU to O-DU/SMO. 38 
2.2 Interfaces 39 
The M-Plane interface is defined between the O-RU Controller and the O-RU. The protocol stack of the M-Plane interface is 40 
shown in the figure below. The transport network layer is built on IP transport and SSH/TCP, and optionally TLS, is used to 41 
carry the M-Plane message between the O-RU Controller and the O-RU. As an option, the O-RU may support the capability 42 
to support asynchronous notifications to be sent using HTTPS. This option enables system optimization when the O-RU 43 
Controller corresponds to the SMO which is operating with a non-persistent NETCONF session to the O-RU. 44 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          20 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
 1 
 2 
 3 
Figure 3: M-plane protocol stack 4 
2.3 YANG Module Introduction 5 
The data models representing the M -Plane are organized as a set of reusable YANG modules. It is also the intent to reuse the 6 
publicly available and generic YANG models as much as possible instead of developing customized O-RAN specific modules. 7 
Refer to the various chapters, Annex D and the repository of YANG models for more details on each of these modules.  8 
2.4 Security 9 
The M -Plane provides end to end security as a mandatory feature , see Table 1. M-Plane security shall support  SSHv2 in 10 
accordance with RFC 6242 [5]. TLS 1.2 in accordance with RFC 7589 [41] may be optionally supported. TLS 1.3 in accordance 11 
with RFC 8446 [42] may also b e optionally supported in addition to TLS 1.2.  RFC 6242 [5] and RFC 7589 [41] provide the 12 
procedures for interoperability with NETCONF implementations. If there are multiple NETCONF sessions established with a 13 
single O-RU, either SSH tunnels or TLS connections may be used and each session should be established over a separate SSH 14 
tunnel or TLS connection . An O -RU shall support sFTP based file transfer over SSH.   An O -RU that supports optional 15 
NETCONF/TLS shall also support FTPES based file transfer over TLS . For the O -DU, the operator may use SSH, TLS, or 16 
both.  It is recommended that operators use NETCONF/TLS and FTPES in production networks. 17 
 Note that TLS versions 1.0 and 1.1 have been deprecated by the IETF [55] and shall not be used. 18

Table 1- M-Plane Security 19 
SSHv2 may be used to perform SSH server host authentication, key exchange, encryption, and integrity protection. It also 20 
derives a unique session ID that may be used by higher -level protocols. The end point (SSH client) authentication should be 21 
done as per RFC 4252 [6]. Chapter 3 describes the authentication approach based on username and password as well as based 22 
on X.509 certificates. 23 


                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          21 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
The SSHv2 transport level security (encryption algorithms, data integrity algorithms) shall be based on RFC4253  [7]. As per 1 
aes128-ctr shall be the mandatory ciphering protocol, and rest of the ones listed as optional. For data integrity, hmac-sha2-256 2 
shall be the mandatory algorithm, and rest of the listed algorithms shall be optional. Public key-based host authentication shall 3 
be used for authenticating the server (RFC 4253) by the clients, and username/password based client authentication shall be 4 
done by the server as part of the SSH session establishment. The O-RU shall support the host key algorithms and key exchange 5 
methods defined in section 10.1 of RFC 5656 for securing the Secure Shell (SSH) transport.   6 
Note, in order to ensure backwards compatibility with equipment supporting earlier versions of this specification, an 7 
operator and vendor may agree to use one of the optional ciphering protocols. 8 
As an additional option, both client and server may implement authentication based on X.509 certificates. With this option, 9 
RSA 2048 bit shall be supported for the Public Key algorithm, aes 128-ctr shall be supported for the cyphering algorithm and 10 
hmac-sha2-256 shall be supported for integrity algorithm. 11 
Note, the above specification will be replaced with a cross reference to the O -RAN Security Task Group Guidelines 12 
once such is published. 13 
TLS 1.2 based on RFC 5246[38] performs mutual authentication, key exchange, encryption, and integrity protection to ensure 14 
trusted communication between the NETCONF server (O-RU) and the NETCONF client (O-DU or SMO). NETCONF 15 
implementations may support X.509 certificate-based authentication using TLS 1.2 based on RFC 7589 [41]. When X.509 16 
based authentication is used, NETCONF server identity is based on RFC 6125 [40] and NETCONF client identity is specified 17 
in RFC 7589[41].  18 
TLS 1.2 implementations shall support the following TLS Cipher Suites with SHA -256 and AES Galois Counter Mode in 19 
accordance with RFC 7540 [42] and 3GPP TS 33.210 [46]: 20 
• ECDHE_RSA_WITH_AES_128_GCM_SHA256 as defined in RFC 5289 [47]. 21 
• DHE_RSA_WITH_AES_128_GCM_SHA256 as defined in RFC 5288 [48].   22 
 23 
It is mandatory that TLS implementations follow the rules on allowed cipher suites specified in the O-RAN Security Protocols 24 
Specifications [56] . Implementations may include additional TLS cipher suites that provide mutual authentication and 25 
confidentiality as required by NETCONF in RFC 6241 [3].  Only cipher suites with AEAD (e.g. GCM) and PFS (e.g. ECDHE, 26 
DHE) and recommended by IANA [45] may be optionally supported. The disallowed cipher suites in RFC 7540 [49], Appendix 27 
A, shall not be used.  The vendor and operator need to be prepared to replace integrity and/or ciphering algorithms if the current 28 
algorithm in use is compromised or deprecated. TLS 1.2 shall follow TLS profiling defined in 3GPP TS 33.210 [46] section 29 
6.2.3. 30 
Operators may select the authentication mechanism and protocol to use as shown in Table 2. 31

Chapter 3 “Start up” installation  33 
This chapter provides the overall start-up mechanism from the power-on of O-RU to available in service. 34 
Pre-condition:  35 
- Power-ON for O-RU/NETCONF Server or O-RU restart operation.  36 
 Power-ON for O-RU controller/NETCONF Client(s) and/or pnfRegistration event-collector. 37 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          22 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
- Physical interface(s) is(are) connected. 1 
Post-condition:   2 
- O-RU is ready for the radio transmission to the air on at least one carrier if packet transmission received from O-DU  3 
- O-RU is ready for the packet transmission to the O-DU if radio reception received at the air on at least one carrier. 4 
- At least one O-RU Controller/NETCONF client with either “super-user” or “hybrid-odu” access privileges can control the 5 
carrier configuration of the O-RU/NETCONF server in O-RU. 6 
 7

Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
 1 
Figure 4: Overall of Start-Up Installation 2 


                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          24 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
 1 
At the power-on of O-RU or following an O-RU restart, the following procedures are performed. 2 
1. O-RU performs M-Plane transport layer resolution (DHCP, MAC, VLAN, IP, etc.) and recovers IP address(es) of O-RU 3 
controller(s) and/or pnfRegistration event-collector. 4 
2. O-RU begins synchronization of the O-RU against a Primary Reference Clock. (Note: step 2 may be in parallel with step 1 5 
for some O-RU implementation.) 6 
3. O-RU performs NETCONF Call Home to discovered O-RU controller(s) and/or performs pnfRegistration to discovered 7 
event-collector. 8 
4. O-RU controller performs SSH or TLS connection establishment. 9 
5. O-RU and O-RU controller perform NETCONF capability discovery. 10 
6. O-RU controller performs optional provisioning of new management accounts (typically only performed once during pre -11 
staging) 12 
7. O-RU and O-RU controller perform supervision of NETCONF connection. 13 
8. O-RU controller performs retrieval of O-RU information. 14 
9. O-RU controller performs SW management. 15 
10. O-DU performs CU-Plane transport configuration 16 
11. (opt) O-DU performs LBM configuration (CU-Plane over ETH) or enables UDP Echo (CU-Plane over IP). 17 
12. (opt) O-DU performs initial C/U-Plane transport connectivity checking between O-DU and O-RU. 18 
13. O-RU controller retrieves the O-RU delay profile from the O-RU. 19 
14. O-RU controller performs U-Plane configuration between O-DU and O-RU. C/U-Plane transport connectivity between O-20 
DU and O-RU is configured as part of this step. 21 
15. O-DU optionally performs C/U-Plane delay measurements between O-DU and O-RU if the O-RU supports it. 22 
16. O-RU controller performs Fault Management activation  by creating subscription to fault management event stream if O-23 
RU controller has not subscribed to default event stream. Additionally, O-RU controller retrieves list of O-RU’s active alarms. 24 
17. O-RU controller activates performance measurement (if required at start-up timing). 25 
18. O-RU controller retrieves O-RU state, including synchronization information, from O-RU. 26 
19. O-RU controller configures the O-RU operational parameters. 27 
20. Service available. 28 
Note, the synchronization procedures started in step 2 needs to be completed before service is available. 29 
Note: periodic CU-Plane connectivity check is not considered as the part of start-up. Once configured in start-up phase, 30 
CU-Plane connectivity check can later be performed periodically and at any time in run-time. 31 
This chapter mainly covers 1 and 3 to 7 as sub-sections. 32 
Cross Reference of other chapters: 33 
The detail of 2. Synchronization management is described in Chapter 10. 34 
The method of 8 and 17 retrieval of O-RU information is described in Chapter 6. 35 
The detail of 9. SW management is described in Chapter 5. 36 
The detail of 12. C/U-Plane transport connectivity checking between O-DU and O-RU is described in Chapter 4. 37

3.1 Management Plane Transport aspects 7 
This sub-section provides the M-plane transport establishment scenario between O-RU and O-RU controller(s), such as O-DU 8 
and/or SMO. The transport layer address of M -plane is only the target in this section. Transport aspects of the C plane and U 9 
plane are covered in Chapter 4. 10 
Pre-condition:  11 
- Physical interface is connected. 12 
- When operating in an environment using call -home, the NETCONF server and NETCONF Client(s) have an identical 13 
NETCONF call home port configured, to ensure the NETCONF client listens on the same port used by the NETCONF Server. 14 
Post-condition:   15 
- Transport Layer address(es) for M-plane are known to O-RU and O-RU controllers.  16 
- O-RU is aware of the physical port(s) for M-plane, e.g., if there are multiple ports in the O-RU. 17 
- O-RU is aware of the VLAN(s) to be used for M-Plane, e.g., if VLANs are used in the transport network. 18 
- Then O-RU is ready to establish TCP connection for NETCONF call home and/or for PNF registration. 19 
For the transport establishment, there are the following alternatives: 20 
a) Manual transport layer address configuration in O-RU. This configuration contains the addresses for O-RU and NETCONF 21 
client(s) and/or the event-collector. The method to manually configure the O-RU is out of scope in this specification. Assuming 22 
manual configuration is successful , the NETCONF server shall be able to  recover this configured information and use the o-23 
ran-mplane-int.yang model to communicate this operational-state to a NETCONF client. 24 
b) If IPv4 is supported, DHCP server provides O-RU’s transport layer address information together with the identity of the 25 
NETCONF client and/or the identity of the event-collector. This identity encodes either the transport layer address or FQDN of 26 
the NETCONF client or event-collector. If an FQDN is signaled, the O-RU shall use the DNS server address provided by the 27 
DHCP server to recover the IP address corresponding to FQDN of the NETCONF client or event-collector. 28 
c) If IPv6 is supported, Stateless Address Auto -Configuration (SLAAC) is used to configure the O-RU’s transport address 29 
with the DHCPv6 server providing the identity of the NETCONF client and/or event-collector. This identity encodes either the 30 
transport layer address or FQDN of the NETCONF client or event-collector. If an FQDN is signaled, the O-RU shall use the 31 
DNS server address provided by the DHCPv6 server to recover the IP address corresponding to FQDN of the NETCONF client 32 
or event-collector.  33 
Note, a NETCONF client can receive a hint as to  whether an O-RU supports a particular IP version by using the get rpc to 34 
recover the list of interfaces supported by the O-RU and using the presence of the augmented ipv4 container or ipv6 container 35 
in the o-ran-interfaces module as an indication that a particular IP version is supported. 36 
The O-RU uses the o-ran-dhcp.yang model to be able to expose information signaled by the DHCP server. 37 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          26 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
 1 
Figure 5: Transport Layer Establishment for M-plane 2

Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
Transport Layer interface related information for M -plane contains at least the physical port number, the hardware address of 1 
the Ethernet port, VLAN-ID, local IP address, remote IP address, Default Gateway address and Subnet mask.  2 
In the case of option b) and c), the following subsections are used: 3 
- O-RU identification in DHCP messages from O-RU. 4 
- VLAN discovery aspect for M-plane. 5 
- IP address assignment to O-RU. 6 
- Discovery of address information of O-RU controller(s) and/or Event-Collector. 7 
3.1.1 O-RU identification in DHCP 8 
The O-RU shall identify itself as an O-RU to DHCP servers by using DHCP option(s) using the vendor-class-data string within 9 
the o-ran-dhcp YANG model. If the O-RU supports IPv4, there are two alternatives. One uses option 60 Vendor Class Identifier, 10 
RFC2132 [8]. The other uses option 124 Vendor Identifying Vendor Class Option, RFC3925 [9]. An O-RU implementing IPv4 11 
shall support at least one of these options. If the O-RU supports IPv6, then it shall identify itself using th e DHCPv6 Vendor 12 
Class Option. 13 
DHCPv4 Vendor Class Option: 14 
• Option: 60 15 
• Vendor Class Identifier Option 60: string  16 
The format of the vendor class string shall be configured to one of the following three options:  17 
1. ““o-ran-ru2/<vendor>, e.g., “o-ran-ru2/vendorA” 18 
2. “o-ran-ru2/<vendor>/<product-code>”, e.g., “o-ran-ru2/vendorA/ORUAA100” 19 
3. “o-ran-ru2/<vendor>/<product-code>/<serial-number>”, e.g., “o-ran-ru2/vendorA/ORUAA100/FR1918010111” 20 
DHCPv4 Vendor-Identifying Vendor Class Option: 21 
• Option: 124 22 
• Enterprise number: O-RAN-alliance 53148 23 
• Vendor-Class-Data: the format of the string shall follow the rules defined for the DHCPv4 Vendor Class Option 24 
DHCPv6 Vendor Class Option: 25 
• Option: 16 26 
• Enterprise number: O-RAN-alliance 53148 27 
• Vendor-Class-Data: the format of the string shall follow the rules defined for the DHCPv4 Vendor Class Option 28 
The DHCP Server may use the information when selecting an address pool from which to allocate an IP address to the O-RU  29 
or when selecting which management plane O-RU Controller information to configure in the O-RU. 30 
3.1.2 Management Plane VLAN Discovery Aspects 31 
The O-RU will be connected to one or more Ethernet ports. The transport systems may be realized such that these Ethernet 32 
ports may be configured either as an access port, where untagged Ethernet frames are used, or as a trunk port, where multiple 33 
VLANs are configured. At start up, the O-RU will typically not be able to immediately determine whether its ports are attached 34 
to remote transport equipment configured for access or trunk mode operation.   35 
Once an O-RU completes its boot-up sequence and Ethernet connectivity is detected on at least one of its Ethernet interfaces, 36 
the O-RU starts management plane connection establishment. 37 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          28 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
The O-RU needs to determine whether it is connected to an access port or a trunk port. In particular, when connected to a trunk 1 
port, the O-RU needs to additionally determine the VLAN identity/ies used to support the management plane communication(s). 2 
The VLAN(s) used to support management plane communications can be identified by the DHCP server replying to the DHCP 3 
DISCOVER message, as described in sections 3.1.4 and 3.1.6. 4 
Note, an O-RU which supports IPv6 may infer that a VLAN is not used to support management plane communications if it 5 
receives an IPv6 Router Advertisement without either the “managed address configuration” or “other configuration” bits set. 6 
If the O-RU does not have previously configured management plane VLAN information, the O -RU shall attempt to discover 7 
DHCP servers on all of its Ethernet ports using untagged Ethernet frames.  8 
When the  O-RU has been previously configured with management plane VLAN information, the O -RU may use this 9 
information to optimize its discovery of the VLAN ID(s) used for management plane connectivity . Previously configured 10 
management plane VLAN information includes an O-RU that stores the last VLAN(s) used for management plane connectivity, 11 
and/or an O-RU which has been previously configured with a range of management plane VLANs by a NETCONF client using 12 
the contents of the searchable-mplane-access-vlans-info container that have been stored in reset-persistent memory. The O-13 
RU may use this information to optimize its discovery of the VLAN ID(s) used for management plane connectivity. 14 
If the O-RU does not receive a DHCP OFFER from a DHCP server using untagged frames, or previously configured VLANs, 15 
the O-RU should attempt to contact a DHCP server using the full range of VLAN IDs (1~4094) on all of its Ethernet ports. 16 
The individual VLAN search algorithm used by an O-RU should ensure timely activation of the M-Plane while accommodating 17 
scenarios whereby there may be an intermittent or temporary connectivity problem between the O -RU and the DHCP server 18 
causing no DHCP response to be received on the M-Plane VLAN. The O-RU should repeatedly search using untagged frames 19 
and previously configured VLANs whenever it searches across the full range of VLAN IDs. The O-RU controller is able to 20 
recommend the maximum interval between repeatedly scanning for M -Plane connectivity on the untagged and configured 21 
VLANs using the scan-interval schema node.  22 
For example, the default scan-interval is 60 seconds. If the O -RU takes 1 second to scan an individual VLAN, then after 23 
scanning every 60 out of the full range of VLAN IDs, the O -RU should repeat the scan for M-Plane connectivity on untagged 24 
and configured VLANs. 25 
3.1.3 O-RU Management Plane IP Address Assignment 26 
Automatic IP address assignment for the O-RU management plane can be achieved using different techniques: 27 
1. IPv4 configuration using DHCPv4, RFC2131 [10] enables DHCP servers to configure IPv4 network address(es) on the O-28 
RU. An O-RU implementing IPv4 shall support the behavior specified in RFC 4361 [33], using stable DHCPv4 node identifiers 29 
in their dhcp-client-identifier option. 30 
Note: a network realized with multiple DHCP servers should ensure that their configurations are coordinated to ensure a 31 
common default gateway is provisioned in an O -RU which receives multiple DHCPv4 responses, e.g., when received over 32 
different interfaces. 33 
Note: an O-RU may indicate that it supports configuration of routing information using RFC 3442, enabling static routes to be 34 
used by the O-RU when determining how to route uplink packets, e.g., when the O-RU supports multiple interfaces. 35 
For O-RUs that support IPv6, both stateful and stateless address assignment procedures are supported: 36 
2. IPv6 Stateless Address Auto -Configuration (SLAAC), RFC4862 [11] enables the O-RU to generate link-local and global 37 
addresses. 38 
Note: a network realized with multiple IPv6-enabled routers that support dynamic address assignment is expected to use RFC 39 
4191 to configure the preference of the default route prefixes learnt by the O-RU using SLAAC. 40 
3. IPv6 State-full address configuration uses DHCPv6 , RFC3315 [12] and enables DHCP servers to configur e IPv6 network 41 
address(es) on the O-RU. DHCPv6 is transported using UDP, using the link -local address on the O-RU and a link -scoped 42 
multicast address on the DHCP server.  43 
Note: the above does not restrict the realization of the DHCP server, which may be integrated with the O-DU, may be provided 44 
by the transport system, or may be accessed via a relay. 45 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          29 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
The DHCP server should operate using static bindings, i.e., ensuring an O-RU identified by a particular client hardware address 1 
will be re-allocated the same management plane IP address, e.g., after performing an O-RU reset procedure. 2 
3.1.4 O-RU Controller Discovery 3 
This section provides how to automatically discover the O-RU Controller address(es). 4 
O-RUs that have obtained their IPv6 addresses by stateless address auto -configuration, shall use stateless DHCPv6, RFC3736 5 
[13], to obtain management plane configuration information.  6 
Other O-RUs operating using stateful IPv4 or IPv6 address allocations shall obtain management plane configuration information 7 
during IP address allocation. 8 
The O-RU as NETCONF Server shall be able to recover NETCONF Client information using the following DHCP Options , 9 
RFC8572 [14]:  10 
• DHCPv4 OPTION V4_SZTP_REDIRECT [143] 11 
• DHCPv6 OPTION_V6_SZTP_REDIRECT [136] 12 
These options are defined in [ 14] and are used to deliver bootstrap -server-list information to the O-RU. The O-RU shall use 13 
these options to recover the NETCONF client information using the above IANA defined DHCP options. The  O-RU is not 14 
required to implement the remainder of the zerotouch capabilities defined in [14].  15 
The above DHCP option provided information is encoded as a list of one or more server URIs, of the format “https://<ip -16 
address-or-hostname>[:<port>]” signaled to the O-RU. The DHCP server shall ensure that all NETCONF client information is 17 
encoded with these options, including the optional port information using the IANA assigned ports specified in RFC8071 [15]. 18 
The O-RU shall use the included port information to decide whether to call home using NETCONF/SSH or NETCONF/TLS. 19 
If no call home port is provided, the O-RU shall attempt to call home using NETCONF/SSH. 20 
Other O-RUs which have had their IP address(es) manually configured, shall also have their O-RU Controller(s) manually 21 
configured. 22 
For IPv4, the O-RU may request the OPTION_V4_SZTP_REDIRECT by including its option code in the Param eter Request 23 
List (55) in DHCP discover/request messages. 24 
For IPv6, the O-RU may request the OPTION_V6_SZTP_REDIRECT option by including the requested option codes in the 25 
Option Request Option. 26 
Note, these operations are optional because the DHCP server wi ll already be aware that it is communicating with an O-RU 27 
based on the recovered vendor class option. 28 
To enable O-RUs to operate in legacy environments where the DHCP server has not been enhanced with IANA defined DHCP 29 
options for zero touch NETCONF capabi lity, an O-RAN defined vendor specific option can be used to signal all NETCONF 30 
client information to the O-RU using option 43 for DHCPv4 and option 17 for DHCPv6.  Multiple instances of NETCONF 31 
client information may be signaled, encoded as a sequence of type/length/value fields. 32 
The definition of the types used within the DHCPv4 option 43/DHCPv6 Option 17 depends on the vendor-class option reported 33 
by the O-RU in its DHCP messages. 34 
When a legacy O-RU reports its vendor-class using the “o-ran-ru” prefix, the following types are defined: 35 
Type: 0x01 – O-RU Controller IP Address 36 
Type: 0x02 – O-RU Controller Fully Qualified Domain Name 37 
When the O-RU reports its vendor-class using the “o-ran-ru2” prefix, the following types are defined: 38 
Type: 0x81 – O-RU Controller IP Address 39 
Type: 0x82 – O-RU Controller Fully Qualified Domain Name 40 
Type: 0x86 – O-RU Call home protocol 41 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          30 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
In all cases, the Type is followed by the length, which is the hexadecimal encoding of length of value field in octets , and the 1 
Value. 2 
When Type corresponds to an O -RU Controller IP Address , the value encodes IPv4 address(es) in hexadecimal format. For 3 
example, a single server with IPv4 address 198.185.159.144 will be encoded in an option 43 TLV as 4 
Type 0x81 (or x01 for legacy) 5 
Length: 0x04  6 
Value: C6 B9 9F 90 7 
When Type corresponds to an O-RU Controller Fully Qualified Domain Name, this encodes the string representation of domain 8 
name, using ACSII encoding (i.e., following for encoding used for the domain name in the Host Name DHCP Option 12). For 9 
example, a server with FQDN “controller.operator.com” will be encoded in an option 43 TLV as 10 
Type 0x82 (or x02 for legacy)  11 
Length: 0x17  12 
Value: 63 6F 6E 74 72 6F 6C 6C 65 72 2E 6F 70 65 72 61 74 6F 72 2E 63 6F 6D 13 
The format of the DHCPv6 option 17 follows the format of the DHCPv4 encoding, with the additional inclusion of an Enterprise 14 
Number prior to the TLV option data. The IANA allocated private enterprise number to be used with DHCPv6 option 17 is 15 
53148. 16 
When Type corresponds to the call home protocol, the value encodes whether an O-RU shall call home using NETCONF/SSH 17 
or NETCONF/TLS using the IANA defined ports in [15]. If no call home protocol type is provided, the O -RU shall use 18 
NETCONF/SSH. The format is encoded as follows: 19 
Value 00 -  O-RU shall attempt to call home using NETCONF/SSH 20 
Value 01 -  O-RU shall attempt to call home using NETCONF/TLS 21 
For example, a DHCP server wanting to trigger the call home procedure using NETCONF/TLS will encode the option 43 22 
TLV as 23 
Type: 0x86 24 
Length: 0x01  25 
Value: 01 26 
3.1.5 Multi-Vendor Plug-and-Play 27 
As described in sub-section 3.3.2, an O-RU may optionally support certificate enrollment using CMPv2. 3GPP 32.509 [52] 28 
specifies how the O-RU supporting IPv4 can discover the IP address or FQDN of one or more Certification Authority 29 
(CA/RA) servers using DHCP Option 43. 30 
An O-RU supporting IPv6 and certificate enrollment using CMPv2 shall additionally support the signaling of vendor specific 31 
options using DHCPv6 option 17. The format of the DHCPv6 option 17 follows the format of the DHCPv4 encoding, with 32 
the additional inclusion of an Enterprise Number prior to the TLV option data. The IANA allocated private enterprise number 33 
to be used with DHCPv6 option 17 is 53148 (as allocated by IANA to O-RAN Alliance). 34 
Note, in case of a point-to-point connection between the O-RU and the O-DU, the O-DU needs to provide means for 35 
the O-RU to access the operator CA/RA for the O-RU certificate enrollment at the IP address conveyed as described 36 
above. If the FQDN option is used instead, the O-DU needs to provide means for the O-RU to access an operator DNS 37 
server in addition. 38 
An O-RU shall report any discovered multi-vendor plug-and-play servers using the o-ran-dhcp YANG model. 39 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          31 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
3.1.5.1 Certificate Enrolment 1 
3GPP 33.310 [51] specifies the use of CMPv2 used by base stations to obtain an operator-signed certificate using a secured 2 
communication based on the vendor-signed certificate in the base station and a vendor root certificate pre-installed in the 3 
CA/RA server. While the approach has been defined for provisioning certificates for use in either IPSec or TLS, the same 4 
techniques defined for provisioning TLS certificates are specified to be re-used here to provision certificates for use in 5 
securing the SSHv2 RFC 6187 [31] based M-Plane connection. Hence, the TLS client CA is responsible for issuing 6 
certificates to NETCONF clients, irrespective of whether NECTONF is secured using TLS or SSHv2. Similarly, the TLS 7 
server CA is responsible for issuing certificates to NETCONF servers, irrespective of whether NECTONF is secured using 8 
TLS or SSHv2. 9 
The handling of certificates, including certificate profiles, shall follow the rules defined in 3GPP 33.310 for TLS CA 10 
certificates. In addition: 11 
• when an O-RU generates a certificate signing request it shall populate the Subject Distinguished Name field with a 12 
string that includes the O-RU manufacturer’s name, model and serial number. The exact Subject DN sub-field used 13 
is defined in the operator of the CA/RA server’s certificate policy. 14 
 Note, in future, an O-RAN defined certificate policy may be defined to normalize the sub-field definition 15 
across the O-RAN ecosystem. 16 
Note, there are various characters that may not be permissible in the Subject Distinguished Name Field, e.g., 17 
“:” (colon, hexadecimal character 0x34), “.” (period, hexadecimal character 0x2E), "_" (underscore, 18 
hexadecimal character 0x5F), “#” (hash, hexadecimal 0x23), “£” (pound, hexadecimal 0xa3), “*” (asterisk, 19 
hexadecimal 0x2a) or “”” (double quote, hexadecimal 0x22). Manufacturers that include such characters in 20 
their name, model and/or serial number should ensure such characters are removed before including in the 21 
Subject Distinguished Name Field. 22 
• when transferring messages to the CA/RA server, the O-RU shall use the “port number of the CA/RA server” and 23 
the “path to the CA/RA directory” as signaled using the DHCP options defined in 3GPP 32.509 [52]. If no DHCP 24 
based configuration is received by an O-RU, the O-RU shall use the default port 443 and default directory “/pkix/”. 25 
• The CA/RA server shall include the trust anchor for the operator issued certificate and the appropriate certificate 26 
chains in the initialization response message. 27 
Note, the trust anchor provisioned in the O-RU during certificate enrolment will typically be the same as the trust 28 
anchor provisioned in the NETCONF client(s), i.e., in O-DU and optionally SMO. 29 
3.1.6 Event-Collector Discovery  30 
This section describes how an O -RU automatically discovers the Event-Collector to which it shall send its pnfRegistration 31 
notification. The support by an O -RU of PNF Registration to a discovered Event-Collector is optional and hence this section  32 
only applies to those O-RUs that support this optional capability.  33 
O-RUs that have obtained their IPv6 addresses by stateless address auto -configuration, shall use stateless DHCPv6, RFC3736 34 
[13], to obtain Event-Collector information. Other O-RUs operating using stateful IPv4 or IPv6 address allocations shall obtain 35 
Event-Collector information during IP address allocation. Other O -RUs which have had their IP address(es) manually 36 
configured, shall also have their Event-Collector(s) and Event-Collector Notification Format manually configured. 37 
The O-RU shall be able to recover Event-Collector information using O-RAN defined vendor specific option to signal Event-38 
Collector information to the O-RU using option 43 for DHCPv4 and option 17 for DHCPv6.  39 
The definition of the types used within the DHCPv4 option 43/DHCPv6 Option 17 are as follows: 40 
Type: 0x83 – Event-Collector IP Address 41 
Type: 0x84 – Event-Collector Fully Qualified Domain Name 42 
Type: 0x85 – Event-Collector Notification Format 43 
In this version of the specification, the operation of an O -RU when receiving multiple instances of the Event-Collector IP 44 
Address and/or Event-Collector FQDN information is not defined. 45 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          32 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
In all cases, the Type is followed by the length, which is the hexade cimal encoding of length of value field in octets, and the 1 
Value. 2 
When Type corresponds to an Event-Collector IP Address, the value encodes IPv4 address(es) in hexadecimal format. For 3 
example, an Event-Collector with IPv4 address 198.185.159.144 will be encoded in an option 43 TLV as 4 
Type 0x83 5 
Length: 0x04  6 
Value: C6 B9 9F 90 7 
When Type corresponds to an Event-Collector Fully Qualified Domain Name, this encodes the string representation of domain 8 
name, using ACSII encoding (i.e., following for encoding used for the domain name in the Host Name DHCP Option 12). For 9 
example, a server with FQDN “collector.operator.com” will be encoded in an option 43 TLV as 10 
Type 0x84 11 
Length: 0x17  12 
Value: 63 6F 6C 6C 65 63 74 6F 72 2E 6F 70 65 72 61 74 6F 72 2E 63 6F 6D 13 
In this version of the specification, the operation of an O -RU when receiving an Event-Collector FQDN that is subsequently 14 
resolved by the O-RU to more than one IP address (i.e., returning multiple Address records) is not defined. 15 
The format of the DHCPv6 option 17 follows the format of the DHCPv4 encoding, with the additional inclusion of an Enterprise 16 
Number prior to the TLV option data. The IANA allocated private enterprise number to be used with DHCPv6 option 17 is 17 
53148. 18 
When Type corresponds to an Event-Collector Notification Format, the value encodes in what format the Event-Collector 19 
expects to receive asynchronous notifications.  In this version of the specification, only a single format is defined: 20 
Value 00 -  Event-Collector expects the notification to be signalled using the format as specified in the ONAP VES event 21 
listener specification [36].  22 
For example, an Event-Collector expecting the pnfRegistration notification to be signaled using the ONAP defined format 23 
will encode the option 43 TLV as 24 
Type 0x85 25 
Length: 0x01  26 
Value: 00 27 
3.2 NETCONF Call Home to O-RU Controller(s) 28 
The O-RU aims to have NETCONF sessions with all of the known O-RU Controller(s), either discovered using the DHCP 29 
options defined in Section 3.1.4 , provisioned by an existing NETCONF client , or statically configured.  An O-RU controller 30 
may attempt to autonomously initiate a NETCONF session with the O-RU, e.g., triggered by the pnfRegistration procedure. In 31 
order to support NETCONF clients corresponding to known O-RU Controllers that either do not attempt to initiate a NETCONF 32 
session with the O-RU, or are prohibited from doing so, e.g., because of Network Address Translation limitations, the O-RU 33 
shall call home to all known O-RU Controllers with which it does not already have an active NETCONF session. 34 
If the O-RU is unable to establish a NETCONF session with some of the known O-RU Controller(s), the O-RU shall use the 35 
“re-call-home-no-ssh-timer” to repeatedly re-perform the call home procedure to all known O-RU Controllers with which the 36 
O-RU does not have an established NETCONF session.  37 
Note, the same value of timer shall be used, irrespective of whether SSH or TLS is being used to transport the NETCONF 38 
session. 39 
If the O-RU is unable to trigger the establishment of NETCONF session with at least one known O-RU Controller after having 40 
repeated the call home procedure a total of max-call-home-attempts per O-RU Controller, then the O-RU should perform an 41 
autonomous reset. 42 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          33 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
The O-RU shall use RFC 8071 [15] whereby the O-RU (NETCONF Server) initiates a TCP connection to the NETCONF client. 1 
The port used by the O-RU shall be the one signaled using the RFC 8572 DHCP option [14], else, if no port was signaled, the 2 
O-RU shall use the IANA-assigned port 4334 to indicate that the O-RU wants to use SSHv2 to secure the NETCONF connection 3 
and the IANA-assigned port 4335 to indicate that the O-RU wants to use TLS to secure the NETCONF connection . When the 4 
NETCONF client accepts a TCP connection on the allocated port, it initiates an SSH session /TLS connection  with the 5 
NETCONF Server. Using this SSH session/TLS connection, the NETCONF client initiates a NETCONF session. 6 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          34 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00

Figure 6: Outline of NETCONF call home procedure 2

Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
The O-RU shall ensure that a persistent connection to any NETCONF client with “sudo” privileges is maintained by actively 1 
testing the aliveness of the connection using the keep-alive mechanism defined in [15]. The establishment of NETCONF client 2 
privileges is covered in Section 3.4. 3 
3.3 NETCONF Connection Establishment 4 
The identity of the NETCONF server (O-RU) shall be verified and authenticated by the NETCONF client according to local 5 
policy before password-based authentication data or any configuration or state data is sent to or received from the NETCONF 6 
server.  7 
When using SSHv2, public key-based host authentication shall be used for authenticating the server (RFC 4253) by the clients. 8 
In addition, server authentication based on X.509 certificates may also be provided [31]. 9 
When using TLS, X.509 certificate-based authentication shall be used for mutual authentication between the NECTONF client 10 
and NETCONF server.  11 
Note: SSHv2 based public key-based host authentication requires that the SSH server ( O-RU) public keys are provisioned in 12 
the NETCONF client (e.g., O-DU and/or SMO). As an alternative, RFC4251 mentions that “a possible strategy is to only accept 13 
a host key without checking the first time a host is connected, save the key in a local database, and compare against that key on 14 
all future connections to that host.”. This option simplifies the key management procedure as it doesn’t require to pre-populate 15 
them in O-DU/SMO (SSH client) but obviously at the price of degraded security, therefore the support of this option shall be 16 
configurable and left to operator’s choice. 17 
3.3.1 NETCONF Security 18 
As specified in clause 2.4, this version of the O -RU Management Plane Specification uses TLS 1.2 or SSHv2 for mutual 19 
authentication between the NETCONF server (O -RU) and the NETCONF client (O -DU or SMO). Support for TLS 1.2 is 20 
optional and support for SSHv2 is mandatory as described in Table 2. 21 
If multiple NETCONF sessions are established to an O-RU, those sessions shall be established over separate SSH tunnels/TLS 22 
connections. 23 
3.3.2 NETCONF Authentication 24 
This version of the O-RU Management Plane Specification supports SSHv2 using password authentication method for SSHv2 25 
[6] and client authentication based on X.509 certificates [31] , as well as optional support for TLS 1.2 using X.509 certificate -26 
based authentication. 27 
The identity of the NETCONF server (O-RU) shall be verified and authenticated by the NETCONF client  (O-DU or SMO ) 28 
according to local policy before authentication data or any configuration or state data is sent to or received from the server. 29 
The identity of the NETCONF client (O -DU or SMO) shall be verified and authenticated b y the NETCONF server (O-RU) 30 
according to local policy (X.509 certificate-based or username/password) to ensure that the incoming NETCONF client request 31 
is legitimate before any configuration or state data is sen t to or received from the NETCONF client. The server shall also 32 
perform proper authorization of the client before accepting any request. 33 
If authentication is based on X.509 certificates, for the purposes of user authentication, the mapping between certific ates and 34 
user-name is provided by the SubjectAltName field of the X.509 certificate, which means that the user name is coded in the 35 
subjectAltName. The username is determined from the subjectAltName using the rules defined in RFC 7589. For the purposes 36 
of NETCONF server authentication, RFC 7589 [41] specifies server identity based on RFC 6125 [40]. 37 
Upon initial system initialization, the O-RU is configured with a default account. The specific details of the default account are 38 
to be agreed between operator and vendor. An example of a default user account for account-type PASSWORD is one with 39 
username “oranuser”. An example of a default user account for account -type CERTIFICATE is map type “san-rfc822-name” 40 
with an rfc822-name of “oranuser@o-ran.org”. 41 
The default account may be of account -type PASSWORD, in which case a default password also needs to be defined and 42 
configured in the O-RU, for example “o-ran-password”. 43 
 44 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          36 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
Note: As the default account may be operator specific, this may require that the O-RU provides facilities to configure 1 
securely this default account at installation time (i.e., before the O-RU is connected to the O-RU Controller). 2 
If user authentication is based on X.509v3 certificate during O-RU plug and play, to support zero touch for the first NETCONF 3 
connection, the O-RU shall support the default mapping between certificate and default NETCONF account which will map 4 
any authenticated X.509 v3 certificate to this default O-RAN account. 5 
Note: The trust anchor for O-RU shall be provisioned automatically with online CA server during O-RU Plug and 6 
Play, and it must be same as the trust anchor of the O-RU Controller(s), thus avoiding the need for manual 7 
configuration of the peer trust anchor for O-RU.  8 
The default account is a member of the “sudo” access control group (see Section 3.4 for details of groups/privileges) as it can 9 
be used to create other accounts (see Section 3.3.3). 10 
The identity of the SSH client (NETCONF client) shall be verified and authenticated by the SSH server ( O-RU) according to 11 
local policy to ensure that the incoming SSH client request is legitimate before any configuration or state data is sent to o r 12 
received from the SSH client.  13 
Upon initial system initialization, the NETCONF client can authenticate itself to the O-RU using SSH Authentication, with the 14 
agreed default username and password. 15 
If authentication based on X.509 certificates according to [31] is supported by SSH client and server, the certificates need to be 16 
installed at initial system initialization, or can be obtained through certificate enrolment with operator’s PKI (certificate 17 
enrolment as defined by 3GPP with CMPv2 protocol between the NE and the operator’s CA). 18 
3.3.3 User Account Provisioning 19 
The NETCONF client with suitable privileges may provision user accounts on the O-RU, including the accounts (users) name, 20 
password, group (see Section 3.4 for details of groups/privileges) and whether a particular account is enabled or disabled.  21 
• The name for the user is a string which should be between 3-32 characters. The first character should be a lowercase letter. 22 
The remaining characters should be lowercase letters or numbers.  23 
• The account-type is an enumeration, indicating whether password or certificate -based auth entication is used for this 24 
account. 25 
• The password is a string between 8 -128 characters. Allowed characters in the password field include lowercase and 26 
uppercase letters, numbers and the special characters: ! $ % ^ ( ) _ + ~ { } [ ] . – The password leaf is not present for those 27 
user accounts associated with certificate-based authentication. 28 
• The access control group associated with an account (see Section 3.4 for details of groups/privileges). 29 
• Whether an account is enabled. The YANG model ensures that at least one user account is always enabled on the O-RU 30 
The new account information (user name, password, access group and whether the account is enabled) shall be stored in reset-31 
persistent memory in O-RU. 32 
If certificate-based client authentication is used no password needs to be provisioned. At time of SSH connection, user’s 33 
authorization is done based on the X.509 certificate’s SubjectAltName field that codes the associated account’s name. 34 
When other user account (sudo) is created, the NETCONF client closes existing NETCONF session as described in section 3.7. 35 
Then, the O-RU disables the default account and default account stays disabled over the resets. The default account becomes 36 
enabled when the O-RU is reset to the factory default software by following the procedures defined in subsection 5.7. Any other 37 
way to enable the default account is not precluded as O-RU vendor implementation matter. 38 
The security principle defined in this section shall follow those defined for the default account and default mapping, i.e., the 39 
O-RU Controller shall create a new mapping. 40 
Note: Depending on the EE/CA certificate of the O-RU Controller, the map type can still be specified but with specific 41 
fingerprint of the EE/CA certificate or based on SubjectAltName of EE/CA certificate as specified in sub-section 3.3.2 42

3.4 NETCONF Access Control 1 
This subsection defines the access control for NETCONF clients. Its motivation is that when multiple NETCONF clients (users) 2 
are defined, the NETCONF access control mechanism enables the NETCONF server to limit some operations for one client but 3 
allow full access for another client. In particular, for hybrid access configuration as introduced in Chapter 2, this allows the 4 
privileges associated with the NETCONF client in the O-DU to be distinct and different from the privileges associated with the 5 
NETCONF client in the SMO. 6 
In order to support interoperable access control management, the NETCONF Server shall use the IETF NETCONF Access 7 
Control Model [RFC8341]. 8 
Currently six access control groups are defined per NETCONF session: “sudo”, “smo”, “hybrid-odu”, “nms”, “fm-pm”, and 9 
“swm”. The table below maps the group name to different privileges. Privileges are defined per namespace for read “R”, write 10 
“W” and execute “X” rpc operations or subscribe to Notifications.  11 
Note: when operating in hybrid management, the definition of above groupi ngs does not preclude the NETCONF client in a 12 
centralized network management system from being configured “sudo” privileges  that permit it to edit the configuration used 13 
by an O-RU. However, importantly the operation of the O -DU in those situations may not be defined. For example, an O-DU 14 
when operating with an O-RU which receives an autonomous reset RPC from a centralized NMS may not result in the O -DU 15 
recovering the o-ran-operations:operational-info/operational-state/restart-cause from the O-RU to then determine that an 16 
NMS triggered reset has been performed.  In order to reduce the possibility of such a scenario, it is recommended that when 17 
operating in hybrid mode of operation, the NETCONF client in the O -DU is associated with the “hybrid-odu” privilege group 18 
and the NETCONF client in the SMO is associated with the “smo” privilege group. 19

Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.

3.5 NETCONF capability discovery 5 
The O-RU advertises its NETCONF capabilities in the NETCONF Hello message. The Hello message provides an indication 6 
of support for standard features defined in NETCONF RFCs as well as support for specific namespaces. 7 
NETCONF capabilities are exchanged between the O-RU and the NETCONF client(s). Examples of capabilities include [3]: 8 
• Writable-running Capability 9 
• Candidate Configuration Capability and associated Commit operation 10 
• Discard change operation 11 
• Lock and un-lock operations 12 
• Confirmed commit Capability 13 
• Cancel commit operation 14 
• Rollback on error capability 15 
• Validate Capability 16 
• Startup configuration capability 17 
• URL capability 18 
• XPATH capability 19 
• Notifications 20 
• Interleave capability 21 
All O-RAN O-RUs shall support the XPATH capability, NETCONF Notifications and at least one of the writeable-running and 22 
candidate configuration capabilities. 23 
The NETCONF client uses the get RPC together with sub-tree based <filter> and XPATH based <filter> to recover particular 24 
sub-trees from the O-RU. Please see Chapter 6 for more information on NETCONF based configuration management. 25 
In order to avoid interactions between the operation of supervision watchdog timer (see section 3.6) and the confirmed commit 26 
timer (default value set to 600 seconds in RFC 6241), when using the NETCONF confirmed commit capability, a NECTONF 27 
client with “sudo” privileges shall ensure the confirmed -timeout is less than the supervision-notification-interval timer 28 
(default value 60 seconds in o-ran-supervision.yang).  29 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          40 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
3.6 Monitoring NETCONF connectivity 1 
This section provides description of NETCONF connectivity monitoring for persistent NETCONF session. Additional 2 
procedures for O -RUs that support the optional NON -PERSISTENT-MPLANE feature to monitor the commun ication path 3 
between the O-RU and Event-Collector are defined in section 15.6. 4 
When having a session with a NETCONF client that has subscribed to receive the supervision-notification, the O-RU operates 5 
a watchdog timers (supervision timer and notification timer)  to ensure that the session to the NECTONF client is persistent . 6 
The O-RU provides NETCONF Notifications to indicate to remote systems that its management system is operational.  7 
An O-RU controller that has subscrib ed to the supervision -notification is expected to use the <supervision -watchdog-reset> 8 
RPC to indicate to O-RU the O-RU controller is operational. 9 
Note, this supervision is intended to be used with the NETCONF client associated with the operation of the peer to the 10 
O-RU’s lower layer split  and section 3.4  describes which NETCONF clients have privileges to subscribe to the 11 
supervision-notification.  12 
Note, a NETCONF server shall support the operation of individual supervision watchdog timers for each NETCONF 13 
client which has subscribed to supervision-notification. 14 
The privileged NETCONF client is responsible for automatically enabling the operation of the watc hdog timers by creating 15 
supervision-notification subscription. After operation of watchdog timers is enabled - the timers are considered as running.  16 
The O-RU uses two timers, referred generically as watchdog timers, to support the bi -directional monitoring of NETCONF 17 
connectivity: 18 
• Notification timer: 19 
Value: Equal to supervision-notification-interval (default value: 60s) 20 
Operation: The O-RU sends supervision-notification to those NETCONF clients that have subscribed to receive such 21 
notifications. The O-RU sends supervision-notification, at the latest when the timer expires. The O -RU Controller 22 
confirms that NETCONF connectivity to the O-RU is operational by receiving the notification.  23 
• Supervision timer: 24 
Value : Equal to supervision-notification-interval (default value: 60s) + guard-timer-overhead (default value: 10s) 25 
Operation: The O -RU identifies supervision failure operation when the timer expires. To avoid supervision timer 26 
expiration, a NETCONF client who has subscribed to receive the supervision-notification should repeatedly reset this 27 
supervision timer. Such supervision timer reset is considered by O -RU as confirmation that NETCONF connectivity 28 
to the O-RU Controller is operational.  29 
 30 
The O-RU enables dedicated watchdog timers for specific NETCONF client when it receives a <create-subscription> RPC from 31 
a NETCONF client with required privileges. The notification timer shall be started when the O -RU receives a <create-32 
subscription> RPC, but how the O -RU treats the supervision timer  is up to O -RU’s implementation based on the above 33 
definition. After the watchdog timers have been enabled, the O -RU is responsible for  sending supervision-notification after 34 
the expiry of the notification timer. An O-RU Controller who has subscribed to the supervision-notification shall be prepared 35 
to receive the notification at any time when the watchdog timers are running. 36 
The NETCONF client is responsible for sending supervision-watchdog-reset RPC in order not to cause the Supervision timer 37 
to expire, and the O-RU should send next notification timestamp  as next-update-at in reply. (Note : next-update-at is just 38 
informative). In the supervision-watchdog-reset RPC, the NETCONF client may configure new values for the watchdog timers 39 
using RPC parameters "supervision -notification-interval" and "guard -timer-overhead. When the O-RU receives  the 40 
supervision-watchdog-reset RPC, it is re sponsible for resetting its supervision timer and notification timer . When the 41 
watchdog timers are running, the O-RU shall be prepared to receive supervision-watchdog-reset RPC at any time  - also within 42 
supervision timer period. 43 
The NETCONF client can set new value of watchdog timers without receiving supervision-notification from the O-RU. The 44 
new values are taken into use immediately with respect to supervision-watchdog-reset RPC content. The next notification 45 
should be expected not later than at the moment addressed in timestamp provided by RPC reply. 46 
Note, if another NETCONF client has locked the running configuration, e.g., when operating in hybrid mode of 47 
operation, and if the O -RU Controller attempts to configure a new value of the watchdog timer( s) by sending the 48 
supervision-watchdog-reset RPC, then the RPC operation to reset the watchdog timer will succeed, but the related 49 

                        
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ          41 
Copyright © 2021 by the O-RAN Alliance e.V.  
O-RAN.WG4.MP.0-v07.00 
backend implementation to modify the watchdog timer(s) may fail. In such circumstances, the O-RU may use the error-1 
message in the RPC output to indicate to the O-RU Controller that the configuration modification has failed. 2 
If the supervision timer expires, the O-RU will enter “supervision failure” condition, as described in Chapter 11 . If all 3 
NETCONF sessions to NECTONF clients with “sudo” privileges are closed, the O-RU shall immediately disable operation of 4 
the supervision timer. 5
